One result of this flurry of activity was the establishment in 1837 of the government Schools of Design (there were twenty-one by 1849). These schools have not fared well with historians and must stand as one of the most neglected chapters in the history of both Victorian reform and Victorian design. Usually viewed in much the same negative light as Henry Cole viewed the early schools, they remain one of the whipping boys for bad design in the nineteenth century. When addressing the schools, most historians simply repeat Cole's argument that the schools failed to pay attention to practical design and were, in effect, "enfeebled and hardly functioning."

There are a number of reasons for this negative view, the most important perhaps is that Cole, the greatest art politician of the century purposely exaggerated the negative features of the schools, particularly the early ones, in order to gain public support and funding for his

Evaluated in terms of the art-labor training system of the time, these Schools of Design look entirely different. Between 1837 and 1848, over fifteen thousand men and women attended them. Most important, these preliminary findings suggest that we reconsider some of our long-standing notions with regard to art-labor in the age of the factory. Was the only art-labor that took place that found in the Morris-inspired cottage workshops? The picture, as we have seen, appears to have been otherwise.

Mechanization, as Raphael Samuel reminds us, "was a process ter than n event" a pros "neither linear nor smooth but, on he cai ulling inpite ionseal t tb mo rt-boinensi  n bec he tital met skil quistion an tanser po naequate nd ws rtunites.Whereas undr the preindustrial ystem, worker inde lizy osi al too e through a patrimonial and group-controlled apprenticeship, now much anization is "increased dependence upon the dictates of capitalist relations," it is possible that for those workers connected to art-labor industries, the machine generated not proletarianization but rather considerable new art-labor opportunities and new skill acquisition opportunities, both of which may have led some workers down the road of deproletarianization. Perhaps we need to squelch the din of the critic Ruskin and his tribe, refocus our perspective on the workplace and the Schools of Design, and listen to the workers themselves.

# Evolutionary Affinity in Arthur Mackmurdo's Botanical Design Larry D. Lutchmansingh

Although Mackmurdo's botanical motifs of the early 1880s take their place in a long and well-established tradition that goes back at least to designers such as Owen Jones (1809-74) and Christopher Dresser (18341904) and looks forward to ar nouveau tion becomes clear through comparison of Mackmurdo's cover of Cha small embroidered Acanthus hanging (Figure 2), designed by William Morris and executed by Morris and Company approximately three years earlier; observes a strict symmetry about the central vertical axis, a more intricate interweaving of two different vegetal patterns, and a deeper spatial effect, notably aided by the contrasting tones of the brown-gold vegetation and the blue back-Th atal iutn ht pt ii directed focus nor the suggestion of a single source of growth. On the other hand, Mackmurdo achieves a more direct and concentrated expression of organic vitality by use of asymumetrical organization on a single plane, a greater economy of imagery, and a dramatic juxtaposition of alternate black and white bands that sprout from a source near the lower center of the composition in a swinging, rhythmic motion and terminate in three unequal blooms tive feature of this work is the way everything seems to be responding to an equally dynamic, external force. In contrast to this dynamism, only the elongated figures of the peacocks at the vertical margins and the letters and numbers giving details of authorship and publication provide any suggestion of stability.

The principles of organic evolution that Spencer derived from observations of natural phenomena are directly applicable to Mackmurdo's botanical designs: (1) Because matter is perceived in ters of forces ofattraction and repulsion, al things exhibit the aspect of resistance to both dilation and compression. ) Considering the law of attraction and repulsion of bodies, movement s deterined y taction  resistance a thu ly fo a aigh xs.Exa elay s re,or te of action and reaction never brings about a return to a previous state. ## The initial elaboration of historical modeling

The origins of the applied arts ideal of historical modeling occurred in the period between 1730 to 1850. During this extraordinary time, a series of major technological inventions ranging from the steam engine to the railroad transfigured economic and social relations in Europe. By 1850, the inexpensive, easily reproducible products of pla n  As in furniture, glassware, linens, and metalwork, vanished. Machine-produced goods gained dominance in the urban marketl  l oout the  ceated t ci  l . City-oriented tastes favored inexpensive, poorly made, and ultiy  mac  hch p h ey lo o a wauely'nt plid that al e h intended renewal of the hand crafts. As we will see,such was not to te  ntil the   the en cny.

## Historical modeling at the applied art museums

True to his conviction in artistc traditions, Rudol Eitelberger, director of Vienna's Museum of Art and Industry, revolted against the extnt to which mas productin  damaged the s of the apped . In hs 7 esy nblihe Zeit p Hea owork  foreig dess than ncoadepndnt thought degeneracy, leaving the population no choice but to buy inferior local goods or superior foreign crafts.

The ruin of handwork by the debased techniques of modern industry, which so disturbed Etelberger could nly e countered by amoent ializullitns Te der AltenTo this end, Eitelberger and other historically minded theorists of the modeling movement were indebted to Gottfried Sn hi proposal r a musm which would isally demonsrate l e.T  hh Semper, who lived in London during the 1851 exhibition, directed his observations and recommendations to German readers in his 181 essay "Wissenschaft, Industrie und Kunst: Vorschlage zu Anregung nationalen Kunstgefühls" ("Science, Industry and Art:

Suggestions for the Revitalization of the National Spirit in Art"). Like others of the time, Semper had a profound desire to approach modern design problems with an understanding gleaned from historical models. Advancing beyond Schinkel's vision of a universal artistic language, however, Semper viewed the applied arts as piu un enlogl catin i back to the earliest days of mankind.

Semper's 1852 essay "Plan eines idealen Museums" (Plan for an Ideal Museum"), emphasized the importance of the techniques of pton stil Beih ocion t shape natinal chaacer, Semper dvocated n warenes o H ment:"A perfect and comprehensive collection must represent the ple oy   on development. ## The privileging of industrial processes

In the 1890s, Julius Lessing's vision for industrial and technological processes as a creative boundary for the applied arts was one of the clearest arguments for a wholesale break with historical modeling.

Lessing's turn to industrial workings emerged, however, from his earlier belief in the restorative power of models. Thus, well before he ok with the philphy  the historical modlimovement, he s.T tet  ug o L   h Mplhainteal hisou H contnuity on the French Revolution, the Napoleonc Wars, and the cl cte ee cenTh  as  tc wol n it teo theaten cny. It was thus clear or Lessing that n order  master the ts at hand, all lost areas of historical technic must be enlivened.

In his texts of the 1890s, written when he was no longer director of the Berlin Museum, Lessing aggressively substituted a H Influenced more than Eitelberger by the materialist aspects of G S L a  h eoey  p sats hanoHanwould t e complete without deliberate consideration of new materials and would be the growing perception among art theorists and critics

By the 1880s, as the German art critic Heinrich Waentig later observed, it was becoming clear that the concerted attempts by the museum movement to invigorate the artistic quotient of the applied as through historical modeling were not succeedingIndustrialists copied historical forms without sufficient regard for quality, and they reaped immense profits from these mass wares.

So it was that the last and highest goal of the applied arts mment hrtlementpl  pn was not achieved. Quicker than one realized, the modern mass inustrial technologies fshioned new forms or themselvs.Thus, the Renaissance which arose from the 'machine's graces' must be

In the 1890s, Lessing came to the conclusion that historicism had caused the applied arts to become stale and inexpressive of new needs and desires. He charged that historical imitation had failed to yield adequate forms for the modern age and was in fact leading to a sharp antihistoricist reaction:

As long as the fundamental conditions of the time remain y and appears to the later generation only as a conceptless flourih. This laternration s then quicky ry o throw o s death the entire cultural period with which this form is associated.

Lesngar ec i Thi mut mege out o theept  mde though  te h  oatn  a  B ing industrial sector. It also meant a more thorough abandonment of hsl er han  sbtal

Inasmuch as Lessing denied history in the name of industrial logic, his writings represent a breach with the historicism prevalent in texts on the applied arts during the nineteenth century, and the start of theorizing on the applied arts as purely constructive."

For Lessing, it had become obvious that technology and sinceha separated the moden ge from all prir historical as. A   thep  te n l cpx  fn tha it   n ib o th in historical forms." He admonished, "What is now demanded with m s theeecion  the or tht hav pint until now.""In a remarkable reversal Lessing now accused historical modeling of degeneracy.

In thus calling for the creation of new forms to embrace a shifting societal mood, Lessing required that forms must respond to—and not ignore—the concrete manifestations of industrial socinineteenth century revolved around this point: "Because of the m   ul our knowledge of forms to these circumstances." Lessing did not ttechnological or functional prototypes. Nonetheless, his calls for the development of new forms to correspond to new technologies anticipated the theory of industrial types by Hermann Muthesius and other members of the Deutscher Werkbund.

It seems undeniable that Lessing's construction of limits bas on industrial methods and materials was a transfigured— less literal—expression of his earlier endorsement of historic models. Whereas Lessing now deplored direct imitation, he retained inaton d ndsrial producti sst. Coninuity between cal ll t on t rs  thic recentering unrestrained artistic production. The dissimilarity between Lessing and Eitelberger was in the actual make-up of boundaries, and not in a belief in their necessity.

In the 1890s, Lessing's critique had repercussions within the applied arts museum world, and brought up serious questions on the hierarchical character of this recentering of the applied arts. Bruno Bucher, the third director of the Austrian Museum of Art and Industry, felt compelled to address contradictions which had n  e   tn Bu t cntemporary concerns were overshadowed by scholary asprple ts musum fro the hitherto emphas n comprehen o  te oln ba th  .Thn g Bucr'desanding  the t  hich ndustl e n o t in northern Europe. The renewed historicism of the nineteenth century presumably had led now to a similar predicament.

It would be a mistake, however, to conclude that empirical aton ndustral l nnature h ely eplachic modeling as a paradigm for creativity within the applied arts museum movement. Historicist attitudes remained strong within the official museum program, while it was outside this program that antihistoricist attitudes were strongest.® Indeed, Bucher did not follow Lessing's radical utilitarianism. In a compromise position, n all that different from Eitelberger's earler stance, Bucher rgued in 1897 that artistic progress resulted from both the study of historical models and contemporary technologies—the development when necessary of traditions which are at hand.® Although aware of dipancs beeen t histoyan inustrial , Bucher cated a balance between the museum's scientific and scholarly aims at muss  neith chaologial nstitts solely stores for idea-seeking designers.

Like Bucher, Wilhelm Bode, director of the Kaiser Friedrich In his 189 artice Aufgaben der Kunstgewerbemuseen" Tasks of Applidt Mus" the conservative leaning  th copromie siton are apparent and ed Bode o conclude, lik coleoT  thefipli  mu losexy ghe lation thepplrg the te to valble model in l dections.et,a yar later, Bod elt obliged to acknowledge that his views were not universally shared. Man pl arpecally tho  the o tl movements in England and Germany—were opposed to the inteningof old forms and methods of decorating does not correspond to the needs of modern life.

## The social politics of artistic boundaries

The mu ebat s p arg scnovers role that history and technology should play in shaping artistic h  he historic recuperation for contemporary industrial creativity as well the Central European museum program to restore those historical associations, however, we see two mutually contradictory dimensions. On the one hand, museums ingeniously extended their nOn  r t d  t   o y social networks ofindustrial society.

First, the museum program sought a cenral postion in ill o  n mn ts o the err  st  stialmo aen   t moreover, a discursive mil of specl consequence for the D WWn  h p c  l thesoil notions of the museum movement. Because of this constancy, the Werkbund debate of 1914 inverted the nineteenth-century conflict

Van de Velde argued for the value of a free artistic imagination indep oe tiions po tally

In the second case, museums stuck to a unified and fine artse world view. Despite the fact that fncionalist an pou larizing opinions were multiplying rapidly after 1900, association between historical modeling and creativity remained central to the museum agenda. This meant that the new social demands on the museum, represented most powerfully by Lessing's industrial realism but also by the new Volkskunst movement, were not met. Within the Werkbund, the radical utilitarians Friedrich Naumann and Hermann Muthesius revolted against the same sterile formalism that Lessing had seen as the outcome of the applied arts attempt to contain the applied arts by traditional artistic models. In a different vein, as part of the growing nationalist movement in the 1890s, Volk-oriented writers of the Heimat (homeland) movement vehemently criticized the aristocratic and frequently foreign models of the modeling movement as soulless formulas extraneous to the German spirit.

The assumptions behind the historical collections had isolated the museum from the emerging industrial and nationalistic mentalities among those writing about the applied arts. Drawing the wrath of both Industrie and Heimat, the importance of applied arts museums in applied arts discourse diminished markedly in the twentieth century.

The rise and fall of the Central European applied arts museum revolves around the history of is conceptualization of efd te a tpl p zst tblihdetical . Dese p sty the eol  he elin ov Although museum directors introduced new audiences to their activities, the largely aristocratic orientation of their collections refused recognition of the populist background characteristic of much of the new urban population. What mattered to theorists from Beuth to Eitelberger and eventually Bode was the imposition of a monologic vision of aristocratic artistic culture upon the disparate

In Lessn', eistance tthe historicalmoeln move knolede. Nontheless while Lessing rejced the piion Lo pl limited set of new technological developments, he eschewed the diffuse and ostensibly arbitrary historical languages of industrial society. Whereas earlier Eitelberger had associated degeneracy with estrangement from artistic traditions, Lessing now identified it with the harmful effects of historical traditions on the modern age's will tl In cf posed as the negative impetuses for equally different visions of authentic and restorative models.

What these observations lead to is an awareness that scopic eb n s  t pld    n arclat the istemoogical mbivalenc moden socty while ct  ial comments, historians of design might well reconsider the relevance of a lineal design genealogy for the modern era. It may be fruitful to explore more fully the numerous concurrent and conflicting narrative fictions which have competed during the last 200 years to ati

# Early Modern Design in Hong Kong Matthew Turner

Whereas we may turn to historical studies of Chinese art or architecture, for example, and draw on a wealth of Chinese and foi olarsh crpdnghsty Chn es to be seen. There is no history of design in India or in a hundred p  W had ever taken place in the rest of the world, except in the remote sense of the vernacular, or in oases of Western influence.

What distinguishes the history of design from the history of art or architecture is surely the narrowness of its geopolitical compass. The design literature is almost exclusively concerned with the First World, that is to say, with the industrial and commercial development of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) region.

## The development of industry in China's Pearl River delta"

The economic strategy of this region was characterized by first, a nh  i successive waves of foreign merchants brought with them new cl  ext s th dapd the wo' oo to Chn a ue o then e teenth century, Chinese export ceramics were designed in a vast ane of adaptie stylfro rough Southeast Asian kend to from Arabc o Latin and English, interlaced with Chine motis and symbols. The epitome of mimicry was reached when fanciful Westr sg we lny op yChin artists for European markets.

Design for manufacturing industries around the Pearl River delta, therefore adopted a strategy unlike that developed by the textile manufacturers of Ahmedabad, where predominantly Indic designs were produced in varying quality to suit different markets. Ch of design adaptation, labor intensive production, and export orientation also ran counter to the strategies of manufacturers and traders in the West, preoccupied as they were with an integrated expansion of mechanization and empire.

From the perspective of a commercial and military power o m nexp ma mt niInh  c ondustrialization tsethe WeTadtional yste trade and manufacture gave place to a colonial economy that T W foh of goods was soon reversed.

## Early modern Hong Kong graphic design

Hong Kong design, particularly raphic design,also strongly devel-u th s  nhzig the wdt ay o N t  h F Anoer surce fr cercializing tadition s derivd fo the mri y

To follow the history of the Kwan family is to trace a complete history of Hong Kong graphic design. In 1845, the China Trade artist Kwan Chuk Lam, known to the West as Lamqua, settled in Hong Kong. He was the only known pupil of the English painter G. Chinnery and had exhibited paintings at the Royal Academy in H a system of serial production and a division of labor. His descendants, a family that currently numbers more than one hundred, followed this commercial principle, moving into the related fields of photography, lithography, poster design, and advertising. Kwan Wai Nung (b. 18s0) learned Western painting from his family and Chinese painting from Gui Koo Chuen. Kwan was employed as a printer, producing the first five-color lithographic works in China. In 191, Kwan became art director of the South China Morning Post, which he t  15  found the Asiatic Lithoaphc in Press, Ltd. Art and business were wholly compatible to Kwan.

## Early modern Hong Kong industrial design

Hong Kong industrial design during the first half of the twentieth century developed much the same adaptive and synthetic strategies as that of graphic design. The silversmithing of Wang Hing Jeweller and the furniture of You On Co., during the early 1900s, show the same articulation of Chinese, Japanese, and Western imagery, material, and technique. Wang Hing Jeweller's silver matchbox in the form of a dancing bear is part miniature of a contemporary Japanese wine bottle design in detailed chasing as well as modeling) and part Western dancing bear with collar and chain. Equally, the Italianate hn rtng the doldte sm a Siz transforming themselves into Chinese dragons. In the same way, the enameled products or glass lamps of the 1930s not only combine Eastern and Western forms, but also include motifs and details anfo the art   theMidle s na

Until recently, few Western designers worked for such markets, but following the Ottawa Agreement of 1932, products manufactured in the British Crown Colony of Hong Kong could be exported to other colonies (and later to Commonwealth countries) without import duties. Hong Kong designers, as with designers anywhere else, had to work according to the demands and, more important, the limitations of their market." One of the founders of Star Industrial Co., Ltd., a large plastics company, explained that the dictated by customers in Asia, the Middle East, and Africa who would not accept tasteful brown and cream; and the company had te fils po  Much te same stoy wstold  he the founder of Sunbeam Manufacturing Co., which produced flashlights in Hong Kong, beginning in 1930. ## The decline of early modern Hong Kong design

With the decline of the British Empire, the stamp Empire Made became less significant, and the 1950s saw an attempt by the Chinese Manufacturers' Association to expand into new Asian markets. Trade missions were dispatched to Japan, Thailand, Malayaustrala, Singapoe, th hilpp Koreaand Indo c    MHo Kong a vale level ual to that o the est of the Commonwalth. But wider political changes were taking place in the world that would transform Hong Kong design.

Although Hong Kong's domestic exports to China had been ecing si e urn the nyChia  awy ben major trading partner. With the Korean War, the United States effectively sealed off Hong Kong's trade with the new Peoples' Republic. Simultaneously, the Hong Kong government began to acknowledge the fact that Hong Kong was a major industrial society as well as a trading port.

U.S. foreign policy aimed at stimulating industrial and commercial development that was linked to its own economyprticularly in Japan, South Korea, Taiwan, and Vietnam. Hong Kong suddenly became favored by American importers. This relationship was in part facilitated by the Hong Kong government's new Federation of Hong Kong Industries, established in 1960; membership was open to all. Three significant changes followed: First, American trading companies appeared, armed with detailed specifications of designs to be manufactured at low cost by Hong Kong firms. Second, the federation assisted six American specialist designers to set up in Hong Kong in 1961. Soon, they were dominating the local design scene.Third, Chinese traditional crafts, no longer available to America or Europe, began to be produced, with suitablemoifiations, toill the vam.

These changes were not fundamental departures from the manufacturing strategies of the Pearl River delta, namely design adaptation, labor intensive production, and export orientation, but the quality of indigenous Hong Kong Chinese design rapidly declined. Increasingly, the design of export goods was carried out either in America or by Americans in Hong Kong. Local designers could not compete. For one reason, although many of the prewar generation of designers had received a solid art education in Guangzhou or Shanghai the younger generation had had no similar opportunity. Even though the wel-known portrait painter Chan Hoi Ying's Hong Kong Academy of Fine Arts included a Design Department since 15, nd the Hong Kng Tecnical Collge, sponsored by the Chinese Manufacturer's Association, opened a Commercial and Desin Department in 0, they alone cul not sustain the broad program needed to modernize the esthetic ios asymmetry, complex adaptations, sensitivity to the demands of non-Western markets, and achievement of an indigenous yet m Ch de w ally  t yrion olat  tI cpan, such  Tai Pin Carpe, L. fed in  wich l x u pl Bn si pduction o Chinee patterns.Theirchief designer sill recall h cults  a aonsThe  y fashion garments and accessories, wood carving, and other crafts ing of modern plastic and electronic consumer goods, Western concepts of design went unquestioned.

## The Western view of Hong Kong design

In part, the prestige of Western design in the Hong Kong of the 1960s was derived from the West's prevailing belief that the modern style of design was one universal visual language, which had originated in the West and which, as with the English language, should be learned byeverybody else as a key to industrial and commercial progress. That China lacked any useful design principles of its own was a belief that rested on almost two centuries of critical opinion in the West.

In 1793, at the height of the Western fashion for chinoiserie, the failure of the British Macartney Mission to interest the Qing Court in Manchester carpets, Sheffield plates, Birmingham "toys,"and Wedgwood pottery was ascribed by manufacturers to the failure of al h  o  pl o cymanacuresy the midnineteeh cntry British r Fenoni mnt. In Owen Jones ncyclopedic Gramar  at 85) n Hon Kong, locally manuctured products re often described as nstic in desiarseculiaf

Hong Kong workers were capable of producing acceptable ss  hove pi e ade o dess ishfr adThuin 0 he firmn ras was tablshe HongKon y Lonn niue ess"ue  theabl C o   h n .

By this date, Hong Kong products had established a reputation for qality  much o the world Indee, b 5 HonKon man facturers were complaining that Japan was exporting goods with fake Empire Made labels, and that Britain was substituting Made-in-United Kingdom labels on Hong Kong gloves and textiles, even while imposing voluntary restrictions on Hong Kong's textiles exports.

Some manufacturers believed that the Chinese tradition had much to offer modern design. Horace Kadoorie had argued in a business symposium of 1957 for: "an amalgamation of Chinese art and Western productiveness in the development of goods for export h as the plastics industry where at times designers e borrowing forms from the East."

Yet, by the 1960s, the designs and the designers of the early modern period of Hong Kong had begun to disappear as American markets and styles came to predominate. A few designers, such as and became a successful designer in the Western manner. Through 0  ol p

As manufacturing and printing in Hong Kong increasingly came to depend on foreign buyer-supplied design, with specifications that left little room for creative adaptation, the tradition of adaptive design turned to mere copying, leaving many young desirswh n eativ fy.

Perhaps this is the fate of design in Hong Kong, which largely survives on translating the samples and specifications brought by foreign firms. When designs are successfully originated in Hong Kong, they are almost always marketed abroad under

foreign brand names. Few realize that certain products retailed as Braun, Melitta, Philips, Habitat, Pifco, Mothercare, and so on, were wholly designed and manufactured in Hong Kong; nor, is there any indication that anybody wishes this to be known. But, for the most part, local industry, employment, and 22 percent of Hong Kong's gross national product must rely on supplying the First World with whatever the market demands.

# Chinese Modern Design: A Retrospective Shou Zhi Wang

## Introduction

Postmodernism is currently a hot topic in Chinese architectural and design circles. The Beijing International Exhibition Center, a sizable complex for foreign exhibitions, is the first large project in this style. Chinese architects and designers discuss the esthetic details of post modern work, and imported publications on the subject, such as Acu Today y Cha Jnce wey rea by yac titioners and students. In addition, articles on the subject have had widespread coverage in art and architectural magazines in China. In a conference to study and review design projects for resort hotels held in the city of Zhongshang in Guangdong province in the summer of 1988, 60 percent of the projects presented by delegates from allcorners of China were Chinese versions of the postmodern sylWhether on the subject of graphic design, product design, inr des   peol  talkig widey  a nw a Ch :  n  The s  eli on Chinese architects and designers that is understandable: China cannot afford to miss out on design as an aspect of international competition, and it is necessary to catch up with everything new. For most Chinese intellectuals, postmodernism is a brand new movement, though it has already had 20 years of history in the Western world.

Without a good knowledge of what has happened and is efforts to modernize.Postmodernism, as a counter-current to the international modern movement, is still a showcase event for the Chin, nd  is pemature to refer to  as a school  ovment. The most crucial questions for  study f Chinee desgn, in my on, involve theChinese modern movement:is  a real movement or just an immature foreign imitation? When did it begin to penetrate architecture and design in China? What does modern Chinese design look like? To begin toanswer these questions is one othe priemotivations o ths paper to ty tutlie the contours of modern design in China.

The Four Modernizations of Prime Minister Deng Xiou-ping, and the ambitious attempt to upgrade China's economy, have received widespread attention around the world. On the surface, all of the Four Modernizations—agriculture, industry, science, and defense—have little to do with the development of Chinese design. However, as a part of economic activity, design has to be brought to the forefront of China's reform of its economy and the restructuring of its bureaucracy. Despite the attitudes of officialdom, in the past decade the demand for new design has accelerated to a previously unbelievable extent. New apartments, new service buildings, new l rcot.Hd thoan  n roduc being poured onto the market. Foreign competition is fierce, with more and more products, from cars to cosmetics, being imported. There is a Holiday Inn in Guangzhou and a Sheraton in Beijing. Kentucky Fried Chicken faces Mao's portrait in TienAn Mien Square, and the showcases of Sony are a magnet for thousands of pedestrians in Nanjing Road, Shanghai. Without the development of design, all battles to compete with foreign products will be lost. The demand is so strong and urgent that Chinese architects and designers are unable expand the time they send i designing and planningToo often the easiest way for design agencies to complete commissions from clients and make quick money is to copy foreign models.

Viewed from a pluralist standpoint, Chinese modern design or Chinese modernity has several main points:

China did not have a really modern design movement until 1979, although there were some fragmented attempts from the late nineteenth century through the first half of the

twentieth century to use design to modernize the Chinese economy. China simply missed a chance to be modernized.

The reasons why China missed this chance lie in the following facts: China's traditional hostile attitude to all and Qing dynasties; foreign aggression and expansion in China since the 1840s, the worst being the Japanese occupation from 1931 (Manchuria) through 1945 (these wars and occpations totally destroyed any possibility o modernizing the Chinese economy); the mismanagement and malfunctioning of the national economy under the Communist government from 1949 to the late 1970s; and the Soviet Union's influence over the Chinese economy, China fomthe rest f the worlatr it brokeoff elations with the Russians in the 1960s.

China's modernity is now being shaped by several pressures: the high economic rate of development now required by the h i  l e desire among the Chinese people for a better standard of living; and China's open policy, which attracts more and more foreign competition, investment, and imported products, as well as new ideas, new culture, and new problems.

The special nature of Chinese bureaucracy is another factor shaping Chinese modernity. Design education is especially affected by this bureaucratic virus.

The development of Chinese modern design is, or will be, in te  the followi moemiating re mon mechanical andengineering appearance. I economic develpolicy is established to guide national economic develop-

## China's Early Efforts to Modernize

China was not transformed from an agrarian society into a predominantly industrial one until relatively recently. According to Albert Feuerwerker, the transformation was incomplete by the time of World War I. Indeed, if we consider the percentage of industrial output in Gross National Product as the key figure to judge a nation's evel of industrialization,China was still an agrarian sciety even by the end of World War II.

China was forced militarily to open its doors to the rest of wor  a x ar  O W the  hen thecy s o Ci  i  ih suffered deeply from setbacks in wars with major foreign powers: Opm Wars with the Briih,  nonaton with the Fench and several with the Japanese and Russians, and so on. There were also several civil wars which wrought terrible damage on the Chinese economy and social stability, such as the Taiping Rebellion i0h Bo . h cl     e trade; the Chinese Customs and Excise Service was established under British supervision; and some industries, the official armaments factories and civilian small manufacturers, were established along the east coast area and the Yantzu River region.

From the setbacks of these wars, some smart Chinese sholas and overnment officials realized the importance of the "New L ng te nt Fn  th e ot i Jpan.A pro ws intro ut  os the Bai Ri Wei Xi O Hundd Da Refor om bruratin e it was suppressed by the conservative political establishment headed he pr Dogr Ci Xi Sm f the ea t refor were executed, and one, Kang You-i, fed to Japan. Emperor Guang Xu, who supported the reforms, was placed in cutdy n the Forbiden City. Every new isue and experiment raised in the reform was abandoned.

Why has it been so difficult for China to accept something new or foreign? This is probably one of the most perplexing questions facing Sinologists. One important fact is China's traditional physical and psychological isolation from the rest of the world for a thousand years. Siberia, the Gobi Desert, the Himalayan mountain chain, and the Pacific Ocean form a physical barrier for China to the rest of the world. Chinese culture has developed in a vacuum of inadequate contact and interaction with other civilizations. There dynastie, which brought about a magnificent cultural and art boom inChna. But this kind of interchange windled after he Song dynasty, and total isolation from the world was complete again by the time of the Qing dynasty. China retreated into the security of the "Central Kingdom,"stayed in its gilded cage of a brilliant past, and avoided contact with any foreign culture.

Unfortunately, the modern age was opening at the same time China was becoming increasingly negative toward world affairs, knowing less about the world while becoming more suspicious. Over the last 200 years, the suspicion became distilled into hatred veyth foen. Sval po o te dynasty repeatedly refused to communicate with Westerners and Japanese." The Chinese people had all kinds of nicknames for foreigners; "foreign devils" is simply one of the many terms indicating China's heritage of antagonism toward outsiders.

The wars in the second half of the nineteenth century y C   c playing a more important role in business, trade, the stock Ta Ch c in tion remained relatively unchanged until recent times."

ThesyChiheen cnh been throw the Qingdynasty resulted in a fragile republic headed by Dr. Sun Yat-sen in 1911. Dr. Sun resigned from his Presidency several mons after his inauguration,and the new PresidentYuan Shii m .He o for two months and died six months later. Dr. Sun was re-selected nt u heal o  p fo the policl iy   o mnt nd r or in northe China. He w suced y Chiang Kai-shek.

The Chinese Communist Party was formed in 1921 and beme an incesingly important political fore. For  shor time in the early 1920s there was a honeymoon between the Communists and the ruling Nationalist Party or Kuomintang (KMT). However, between the Communists and KMT continued for several decades.

At the same time, Japanese expansion in China created another a part  orh China w cpd in 3Shahai W     i losses in every sense were enormous. More than 10 million people died, and the economy was destroyed—except for the heavy indus-Ml n. Ater World War I, huge areas of China were in ruins.

With the war's end in 1945, most Chinese were relieved and believed peace could be established. But the civil war between the te v     to Cmui to r th the ng  th oRl i n In hor  cn  he the peace in China, not even a ten-year interval. The conditions for the development of a national industry and economy simply did not exist.

Germany, France, Britain, America, and other Western twentieth century, but war did not have such a sustained and ti.There was sill the architectural and design work of Walter Gropus, Mies van der Rohe, Frank Loyd Wright, Le orbusier, Th Vn Deg nd Aa alt Why h' China p   hs  ? l  h y t  n c a few other Chinee cites enjoyed a short economic boom during fter World War  in which some industris developed but ths was cut short by the Japanese occupation of 1938. In the whole period of unrest from the mid-nineteenth century until the 1950s, China had no possibility of developing a national industrial base or of embarking on major construction projects. It is, therefore, understandable that Chinese modern desgn whether architecture or product design, did not emerge in this period.

The first effort to establish modern industry in China began with the modernization of the armaments industry. As Feuerwerker points out, the desire to acquire Western military technology was of the themes of the decade of theTug-chi Restoration,"the Zen Guo-fan (1811-1872), commander of the Hunan military district and ised in Westen metho ins dee to impo China's eeness arly  8 Zen tblih mall als in J pince. I 8 h pen  rseal n hipyard  Aqu Anhui province. Zeng's principal industrial project was the Jiangnan Arsenal at Shanghai in 1865, established by himself and Li Hong-zhang (1823-1901), then acting governor at Nanjing. The as well as rifles, cannon, gunpowder, and cartridges.

To train the qualified personnel necessary to run these enterpriss a new teaching istittion was t up in Beijing in 18The cuss offered incuded astronoy, mathemati, chesty, byogllugaty lal S Chne stdents were o snt to America  ohr Western countries to master the secrets of Western technology in order to uerpin the efforts t el-strehening"that Zeng and Lihad d In Ci oy eo the ff  nz are known as the Yang Wu Yun Dong or Foreign Affairs movement.

In addition to the defense industry, other areas of manufacturing were started: cotton spinning and weaving, silk reeling. fabric dyeing, pottery and glass, soap and candles, matches, leather go, cement, paper making, four milling and ce husking tbacco and cigarette, olpresing and sundy food products. In ition, beginnings were made on establishing the necessary na stuure: steaships, telegrah ines, coal and old mines ailroads, and electricity production.

There was virtually no design activity in a specific sense in any of these early industries. In 1895, the Treaty of Shimonoseki, which concluded the war between China and Japan, gave the Japanese and, through the operation of the most-favored nation clause, all foreigners the right to lih macurninduti n Chi. Manufacturing continued to expand in range. In the mid-1930s, a small factory in Shanghai produced the first Chinese bicycles on the prototype of the British Raleigh model of 103. ## Modern Design in China

Fnl mainland China.

The foundation of the People's Republic of China in 1949 marked a new communist, socialist stage in Chinese history. Immediately after he Chinese Communist arty took power, a large-scale reform and remodeling of the national economy was carried out. Its main elements were:

Removal of all foreign businesses and interests in China. The foreign companies and other enterprises were taken over and confiscated by the Chinese government. All foreignbusiess a tra werecntolled by the ov ment. All relations between private Chinese enterprises with foreign partners were eliminated.

Confiscation of all Chinese private monopoly enterprises (known in Communist political terms as "bureaucratic capital"), which were nationalized and controlled by government departments of either industry or trade.

Elimination of "guilds" in rural areas—small syndicates of trade in rural China, which had controlled most business in rural markets before 1949—by having private property socialized. The aim was for the government to control the rural market.

Building up a network of national department stores and national cooperative supply centers. This network finally became the only business and trade organization in all of China.

Financial and monetary affairs controlled by the central government. The currency, commodities, and other products were allunder the strict control of the governument. Having taken all these steps, the Chinese Communists turned the Chinese market into a highly controlled, highly planned supply and distributing economy based on rationing.This effort temporarily eased the chaos in the market but merely postponed economic crisis.

From a free market to a centrally controlled one, China has followed four main phases of evolution: 1949-56, transition period; 1956-58, formation of a centralized Chinese market; 1958-79, a cumbersome and poor national market; 1979-present, period of economic reform.

## 1949-56, transition period

During this period, the Chinese market had two parallel lines of development: co-existence of public and private business. Because control of national economic power had yet to be consolidated, the government was unable to carry out immediately its plans for nization existed at that time: nationalized business, cooperative businesses based on a contract between the government and a priva o unr e ctrol  the e my private companies formed before 1949 and left untouched for the time being (most located in eastern, coastal China in ports such as Shanghai, then the largest business center inChina),small private busss smal worshos, corshos rstuat private salespeople and shopkeepers.

Within the six-year period, the Chinese Communist Party n ly colle he cnal v  e ba ol  n tliz z enteps  ltceul xpnson  l n  u   t bei  n ing their further development, and eventually transforming them into either a cooperative or a nationalized form. This effort to have the whole Chinese market nationalized and centrally controlled was partly finished by the end of 1955. This prepared the stage for the e plmn i ss

## 1956-58, formation of a centralized Chinese market

A government-initiated movement of redemption or buy-out of all private business throughout the country was carried out in 1956. It panh  al — y m  h oveTe eto ompensatin i nn ndi i   l   pd the s the national bourgeoisie on the money value of their assets for a and commerce into joint state-private enterprises." By the end of publicly owned except for a limited number of small businesses, alugh the later were tighy conrolled by markt conol depart ments that functioned across the nation.

Chinese productivity at that time was poor. A self-sufficient rural economy was still thriving across the vast rural area of China. The gong se hi ying movement brought very little improvement to the Chinese people. It created a highly controlled and nationalized economy and market, which temporarily relieved problems in the chaotic domestic market. However it also began to eliminate a dynamic and competitive market and put a heavy financial burden on the government because it had to subsidize the supply of commodities used in everyday life.

## 1958-79, a cumbersome and poor national market

After 1956, a few private shopkeepers still existed. However, in 1958, Mao Tse-tung announced the concept of a state monopoly of t marT w pr  opn ff   ena h within an impossibly short time period According to Mao's idea therefoe, dered tht all su businses  closd own an cbe ocny e basr of trading organization:

The nationalized trading organization (e.g., nationalized department stores, nationalized Xing Hua bookstores as the only bookstore chain in the whole of China, the Chinese People's Bank as the only bank in the country, etc.).

Nationalized Gong Xiao He Zuo She—a supply and marketing cooperative responsible for supplying productive tools and daily commodities (e fertilizer, agricultural machinery, etc.) to farmers in all of China. This market was proudly proclaimed by the government as a "socialist

united market." In it, commodities were distributed by a system of rationing. There was no competition and only one contriesChinas economy developedn solation.Te 0 s tos enic polics and political uheavals, China' th T mit ab t Th throughout the 1960s and 1970s Because demand for a yT     ll consumers was quantity—the more the better uality nd design were less important. A poor, underdeveloped tehe  e lack of design and design education in China from 1949 until the late 1970s.

## 1979-present, period of economic reform

The disastrous Cultural Revolution (1966-76) finally ended with the death of Mao in 1976, followed by the trial and imprisonment of the Gang of Four." Soon after, China again started an economic reform effort to transform its backward economy into an advanced one. Aug  is possibe here o iscuss detai  the eorm some of the main issues which have influenced the market and design are:

Pemitting limitedprivate wnership n ncain and service-sector enterprises.

Splitting the power of management in all enterprises between the communist leadership and management leadership. More operating control is given to the latter; the professional leaders ofbusinesses and the executiv power of communist organizations in enterprises has been correspondingly diminished.

Introducing in 1988 bankruptcy law for nationalized and cooperative enterprises.

Disbanding People'Communes in rural areas andgiving land back to individual peasants, who may use their "own" land for 50 or more years, dependent on the contract signed th e h

Partly relaxing the former fixed prices of commodities. The pc  banecsits sc cking ment. The oal is or a more ynamic consumer market that ille t  tl and quality. Changing the old and stagnant rationed market into a modern one is the aim of the effort.

Partly opening Chinese markets to foreign trade, foreign investment, and joint-venture projects with foreign parts. z  t ha  h  S Economic Zones (SEZ) to attract foreign investment and ibeal er  pefeentil eatmntReall t   Sh my introduction.)

Importing many high-technology, industrial products, transportation vehicles, machines, and so on, in order to boost the Chinese Four Modernizations. This requires encouraging Chinese exports to provide the necessary foreign currency.

Reducing the defense budget and retiring one million men from the army. The funds saved have been invested in economic development.

Creating a more liberal political atmosphere with fewer political initiatives from the center. The authorities understand that a stable and peaceful political atmosphere is important to develop the national economy.

The cnc h  nhn t decav been ablociviy iger t ely be the  ttig hierehin a o even the brand of products. Competition among businesses is ig mo inten nd aAll hechan have att tte h  th il lems in the reform process. Many contradictions remain untouced some of them having persisted for a long time. The most notable contradictions and problems of present-day China are:

The contradiction between the political structure and the changing economic structure. The Communist Party is always placed above the three branches of government, so China is still a country governed by a group of Communist pitcal leaders rather than b the ule f aw. The polic o t  l plicl ladhe than  l  el legislative bodies.

As the key part of Chinese socialism, public ownership is still adhered to by the central government of China as well t Communist ary.The whole reform f the eonomic foreign, cooperative and nationalized) existing on the basis of a "public ownership" model fixed by the government. With ths publicownershiporcentralized national con Th   oc sm a li a government subsidy, and a private one dependent on market competition. The goods subsidized by the government ae enelly ower n pre buo lmite vaiabilty controlled by a group of bureaucrats.Obtaining public proucts" from the hands of bureaucrats and selling them on the open market is very profitable. Consequently, corruption and bribery are widely prevalent now. There is no way to stop this corruption in China except by random checks and political education of officials.

The country as well as markets are governed by political leaders, not by law. Because market regulations can be arbitaily sbec to dicl hange, the maret lacsa k element of healthy development: a good legal system. Red tape, low efficiency, and sudden changes of economic policy and market regulations are reflections of this political situation in China.

Bec pblishi ill the cre l Whenever citizens earn some money, there is no way for them to invest it to make more. Spending t on commoditie, which they may or may not need, is the only outlet for Cth of savings on commodities, whether needed or not, has increased even more-in order to maintain the value of avigT h  rcase ws partiuy acute in 1987-88 and brought on a nationwide panic. In addition, government employees in China, on low, fixed salaries, have been especially hurt by inflation. The psychological effect of all these troubles has been to throw serious doubt on the reform program.

The government maintains an artificially high exchange rate o the Chinee currency to foreig currencis, but n This creates a lively black market or currenc in biites in China.

Confusion in foreign trade results from its being controlled mainly by foreign trade departments at either central government or provincial level. After 190sme independent rghts for foreign trade were given to some local nationalized companies or local governments. The channels of imports an exports are also overlapping Most authorized foreign trade agencies are government bureaus that have no expericompanies considering business with China.

In the case of the "open policy," the government often tends mix tial sss ithgcl . It  from the West technology, science, management, and "good u the  thn The only standard is the subjective ment of the officials involved. This creates another kind of confusion and chaos."

## Graphic Design

Graphic design in China is called Zhuang Huang, or decoration. A  veoment  0  paatd  t o   H nzed as Sh Ji Zhuan Huang, or publication desin. Publication considered to be important means of propaganda. All private lh w ba n the arl  heon u its own publishing houses. Each province had its own People's Publishing House, which was authorized to produce important opecalst publishing houss were stablishe Books on scince tenology art, music, literature, architecture are published by presses directly responsible to the relevant ministry at national level. For example, the Architecture Publishing House is under the Ministry of Construction; the People's Fine Art Publishing House, the People's Music Publishing House, and the People's Literature Publishing House are all under the direction of the Ministry of Culture. In all these presses, graphic designers are in charge of designing books. Their work includes cover design, illustration, lettering, layout, typography, and logotypes. In publication design, Chinese designers have had some significant achievements in the past four decades, winning major prizes in international publications exhibitions. This is a reflection of the fact that in China publiha  i   ove he houses have had adequate budgets. Without financial problems to inunce their work, designers were able t do a better j.

In commercial graphic design it was another story. Commercial graphics were considered a symbol of "Western lifestyle" and accused of "encouraging unnecessary purchasing and waste of natinal resources."Throughout the whole perd from the 1950s to the 1970s, they were deliberately ignored by the government. H cies were formed under the Gong Shang Guang Li Ju, or Industrial and Commercial Supervisory Bureau. The term "advertising"in Chinese is Guang Gao, which means "announcing to the public." It has no commercial connotations at all. These agencies became the places where the government commissioned political billboards and p, an ptits Maand he political lers.

Managers and designers of these agencies interviewed by the author in recent years include Chen Liang of the Shanghai A  Rn   e Bei Cpd an iin  h Gn vei Company. In the 10s ad 100 perent of the wor ws h t guide the people o move forward; the foreround and back b being sent t thedvertipans.The  theat was to copy these designs onto large billboards.

Commercial advertising in China at this time was very limited. All news media were mouthpieces of communist propaig. Politicl billr n he sreet w coe a mes o eucating the people;commercial versions were a means of seduci them. There was, therefore, no adequate medium for advertisin. In addition, because of the shortage of most commodities and the existence of a demand economy, there was little need for most enterprises to advertise.

In some special cases,commercials had a limiteddevelopment and were permitted by the government. For example, in the mid-1970s, there were dozens of commercial billboards for the

Guangzhou Export Fair. But these were always overshadowed by hu poll bilr ith  litl saThy place where commercial advertising had no problems was on the Th os  the comercil posters was y  ersment the early 190s. However, for export commodities, packaging was necessary and important. Without good packaging to satisfy international expor. ## Product Design

Product design was less developed than all other forms of design until the 1970s. Poor productivity, innumerable people, and endless political chaos brought a common factor to life in China: a shortage of salt and rice were in constant short supply. Rationing coupons, first mboh i e next two decades. (Coupons for rice, cereals, cooking oil, and meat al  I io q   u    D pon n m e for two or three decades without any change.Thermos bottles, which are a major item in Chinese homes because most houses and apartments do not have hot water, remained unchanged from the 1930s l and bicycles were other products whose design never changed.

The Chinese motor vehicle industry was first established in the late 1950s. The need for a motorized road transport vehicle was n y in Chanun, Jiling province in Manchuria in . The tSvit Unn AZ t e  t ax Gky GAZ truck, which at that time was iporte on the ne-cent ill Ul te ideCha pne and North Korea. This demand forced production higher, and design or styling was unnecessary under such circumstances. The J es  bok he For Mo  190 continuous production.

There was no competition in the Chinese domestic market be the ovet ha  vy si mpot pol r os. The only exceptions were some GAZ-69 jeeps from Russia and Rumania, limited production cars such as the Babeda (Victory) from Russia and Poland, Tatra trucks from Czechoslovakia, and buses from Hungary and Czechoslovakia. A Chinese tractor, the Dong F Ho h  F Henan province, and bicycles such as the Yong Jou (Phoenix) produced by the Shanghai bicyce factory had similar design histories as the Jiefang truck: no change for years.

In most Chinese manufacturing, engineers were generally responsible for everything, from mechanical function to appearance. Sometimes artists would be called in to decorate and beautify products. Such artists who work in factories in this way are still known as Mei Gong or "art workers".

China began to make its own cars in 1958 with a model h D e Academy Arts and Craft's were asked o decorate the car, which n    nT were in the shape of traditional Chinese lanterns, the hood ornament was a dancing golden dragon. Yet, although reminiscent of was the precursor of modern Chinese automobile design."

In 1962, China produced its first limousine, the Hong Qi R Flagt the First Auto Factory in Chanun. It was heaviy derived fro the Russin Seagul mousine a standard cr for senor officials and dstinguished foreig uests. It had a V-8 engine po gss windows. At this time, the it with the Soviet Union becg r w  the pon  the Ho  ws T al  0 depending on the level of government orders. All American presidents visiting China have used the Hong Qi, which was the official ice or tvisirs.Pouion w haltd in 1, b Chinese leaders were no longer satisfied with its quality.

The first Chinese factory devoted to car production was set up in Shanghai in the early 1960s. Its initial product was named after the city, the Shanghai. It was shabby looking, with a fyln guros dn uhit n b most important, it was an original Chinese design, by engineers from the of an art nouveau-type Russian Volga car. The front bumper was chromed and heavy, as were the radiator grill and other exposed partsThe most ppular color was ligt apple reen.

In the early 1970s, a new model appeared, which eliminated most of the art nouveau detail giving a simpler look. The form was boxier. Even the contour of the lights was changed from round to square. However, because no designers were involved in the project, the car was rather like an assembly of jigsaw puzzle pieces, with each piece derived from some part of foreign cars. The Shanghai had a very bad maintenance and repair record, and production e in the ary 180st prent, the Shanga  fcoy joint-venture manufacturer with Volkswagen. It produces a small VW Shanghai-Santana and assembles some Audis."

China now produces many kinds of trucks, jeeps, and military vehicles. These include the Jiefang truck from the First Auto Factory; the Dongfeng (Great Leap Forward) truck from the Second Automobile Factory in Shiyan, Hubei province, now the largest automobile manufacturer in China; the Hunghe (Yellow River) tuck based on the Czech Skoda heavy-load truck, by the Jinan Auto Factory in Shandong province; and the Hongyian truck by Chongqing Auto in Szechuan province. Of these plants, the first two mentioned produce engines which are supplied to the other plants.

The main Chinese jeep producer is the Beijing Jeep Factory, c  iBi fhe hc  be i-s  ws used by Mao to parade his Red Guards during the Cultural Ron y war there as aid from China. The Beijing was designed on the model of the Russian GAZ.-69 but with a different front end. A modified version is still the main jeep for the Chinese army.

## Development of Chinese Design Since 1979

The Chinese market has changed rapidly since 1979, when the Chinese government introduced its ambitious plan of economic e thdmarket ationmanhier than suppy new consumer market is appearing. As a result, demand from cnmers r better-desined, hig-qualitycomodities is rowing. This is the background to recent developments in Chinese design and design education. Because many problems in the o the development of Chinese design is uneven and unbalanced. Of adeg tiit vertising desg interor desg hav a comparatively faster development. Product design, or industrial design, lags far behind.

## Advertising

Until 179 there were very few dverising agences, and all were n big cities. Shanghai, a city with a population of Il million, had two agencies, the Shanghai Advertising Company and the Shanghai Decoration Company. There was just one in Beijing at that time, the Beijing Advertising Art Company, responsible for making all the poliical billboard in downtown Beijigincluding the portraits o Mao that hung in the Tien An Mien Square area. Such work was the common pattern for all similar agencies. Commercial advertising was very limited."

After 1979, as business competition became more intense in China, advertising as a means of market promotion came to be srvics is therefore ncreasng and thousans of aencs hav th almost 200 advertising agencies in Shanghai and a similar number in Beijing. Since there were no proper laws regulating commercial came not only through agencies; even some government organiza o   e al iy     t aencs in n attpt o mak eat pofs. Onthe est . She  t the Railroad Bureau of Shenzen.

Many problems remain, however, in building a real advertising business in China:

The Chinese market is still immature and in the unstable process of transformation. Business competition is still not acute enough for high cost, high quality advertising.

There is a lack of regulation and laws to control Chinese advertising.

News media are still considered to be a means of ideologic propagand and are strictly conrolled by the Department of Propaganda of the Comunist Party. Ccial ing  y ia Tnd  neppbo 0 in n On   nt the xim  nhc  ly  i  e . News media have ther own advertising sctions or cmpanies which have a monopoly on creating advertisements for themedThe qualiyowr   d that ly discourages viewers from watching them.

Billboards are limited in number and are under the control of either urban planning or environmental development agencies. For example, there was a total of S0 billboards in Xi'an in1984. Before 199, all 80 were assigned to one agency, the Xi'an Advertising Company. By 1984, there were almost 20 advertising agencies in the city, but due to urban construction the number of billboards was reduced to 0.The aencies soon realized that billboard advertising was not very profitable. There were similar occurrences in other cities.

Most "designers" in advertising agencies have no design qitin S a  ly  p draw but little understanding of real commercial design. So the averae quality evl is ry poor setmes evengly, with negative consumer reaction.

•Foreign advertising is controlled by a few governmentauthorized agenciessu as the GuanongAvertising Company of the Foreign Trade Bureau of Guangdong province. There is very limited flexibility for foreign advertisements to compete with their Chinese opponents in a situation of unequal competition."

There are no direct mail advertisements in China. Both industry and consumers think it a waste.

In this situation, the rapid expansion f Chinese advertising agencies has slowed down, and many existing agencies are seeking to expand into other more profitable areas of work, such as interior design for tourist projects.

## Graphic design

After 1979 the field of graphic design saw remarkable developments in the art of "wrap and pack" particularly in relation to the pann  xpo e te s  ffci i tion were severe. In addition, commodity damage in shippin and losses due to inadequate packaging. Losses in 1983 went into l government established a National Committe for Packaging Tecnology in 192.® It has several independent subcommittees, S  p Several government-sponsored packaging magazines have been iforen package tecly  deehibitns hv been held in China. The government also spends substantial amounts of foreign currency to import technology, machinery, an factories. As a result of this strong government support, China is py ching he e  pagi g nd nology. Design is the weakest point in this development. Design is seen as software, while technology and engineering are viewed as hardware in the eyes of most Chinese officials, who think design is just "beautification and decoration" and therefore logically of less importance.

Many ministris of the central government are also developing packaging for their own commodities and products. In recent years, the Ministry of Health and Medicine has held a series of training classes for its designers of medical packaging, and at the same time has also organized exhibitions of packaging from Weste couni.Smi tivites have been cr t TextI   ant have gained the most support from the government. However, packaging design has fared less well because the main concern of government officials is still better "wrapping and packing." Market competition is still too new to be accepted by most officials.

Packaging design depends mainly upon manufacturing, and h iy  e i ness. Foreign manufacturers, such as Coca-Cola and Pepsi, generally import existing packaging design and therefore do not need Chinese designers. Because Chinese packaging design is a government-riented activity, it exists in a very different context from advertising design.

## Product design

Of allthe forms of design, industrial design or product design is the most ignored. The reason for this neglect can be found in the still-underdeveloped consumer-product manufacturing sector. Although the Chinese market has changed considerably since 1979, the shortage of many consumer products is obvious.According to official government statistics, in 1983 China produced 10.8 million sewing machines, 34.6 million wrist watches, 6.8 million TV sets, 27.58 million bicycles, and 14.88 million meters of fabric. In 1986 China produced 9.86 million sewing machines, 32.35 million bicycles, and 3.8 billion pieces of tableware.

China has attracted more and more attention from the world since its ambitious reforms began in 1979. Its GNP has maintained an annual increase of 10 percent, and people's income has also py . But thee rd  he sar Chinas one billion population. Eighty percent  Chinese are farm oiI   h still an agrarian country, although the proportion of industryin GNP is growing higher. China's main export items are minerals or aicultural pou To fed billnmos   toug sk y any standard, and the issue of quantity is the first priority for most Chinese people.

In ters of the quantity of moen manufacurig Cia hols word recors for prducing the most bicce, watchs,clocks cothes, and so on, all the products necessary to maintain its one bill pTe igy  wat t Mot pruct e silla n fre poot f sveal c  o Wst cn p Raleigh bicycles are kept in museums, but in China they are still being used by millions and are hot items in department stores.

However, a recent phenomenon has attracted the attention of government officials and designers. Millions of products without any styling or design are pouring off Chinese production lines. With income levels increasing and more people having their basic needs satisfied, the poorly designed, old-fashioned products are filling warehouses and cannot be sold. What is just beginning to happen in China is that more people are seeking better-quality and better-looking products; this is indeed a new tendency in the domestic market. According to a news report in May 1987, one million old-fashioned bicycles with little-known brand names, 10 million wrist watches, and 2.6 million sewing machines could not be sold and were sitting in storage. It was the first time in China that goods were not sold out. It indicates that productivity is increasing, which brings more products into the market; but it also shows that the sensitivity of Chine people toward desg nd quality  rown.

There is also another new trend in China's economic boom. o  i forms of business cooperation, such as investment inChinese manu facturing, setting up foreign companies in China, or joint-venture enterprises. The Special Economic Zones have been extended in number and have been the main location for establishing hundreds overseas companis. By the end f 1986,China had attracted 0. billion U.S.dollars in forein investment, iported 1,00 new i.The lttnce uc eln m oln GMA Jeep, Peugeot, Canon, Yamaha, Hitachi, Coca-Cola, Pepsi-Cola, Kodak, and Kentucky Fried Chicken. All have been established since 190 and all are profitable. The product design of these manfacturers is us the same s the mode in overses markes. It is on-Chin esig u ivs thehine peol chance to feel and experience what good design is in comparison with their old and conservative products.

Many Chinese factories have imported foreign assembly lines, machines, technology, and management. Since the factories u or tcnloy the as wy maig prout i ris just change the logos of foreign products or make minor rght regulations. However, there is still a clear resemblance to ig .  pe  hWa R Mh pe vly n Jpan n. which is not popular in Western countries.\*

The examples we have noted illustrate the two tendencies current in Chinese modern product design. One is keeping old desis n t angig the Most product ths cate either well-known brands, such as the Shanghai and Tianjin bicycles, or products still in shortage in the market, such as trucks and automobiles. (The government still strictly controls imports of foreign automobiles to protect and encourage the domestic auto industry.) The second tendency is to copy and imitate foreign SChia c been copid byChin competitors, to use lawsuits to protect their own copyrights.

An interesting example of Chinese violation of international cright is Walt Disne'cartoon characters.As part of Disney' goal stat  n an maetng n  Ch al Tflho l Disney cartoon movies. When shown in 1987, they attracted C ing Chinese manufacturers with copyright violations.

Why is it that China has such a strange situation in design? The first reason s the cy's pecil politicl  econoic ac n I   t W natin  bol  i. Inde te ec o thi eonic stn lasted, the more tho smple yle becme accept s par f the Chine national cac.

Chinese traditional philosophy also hampers people from sekng what is bette oki The essenceoConcin hily e. On e T p teling peopl to pretend to be unselfh in rder to manpulate th sl shoro e i taditional philosophical heritage was exaggerated and usd by Mao in arguing for people who were "unselfish and devoted to comunism.M' effors esulted in an offical verson of tadi tional philosph with a comunist belA comortable le, xurious surroundings, and self-enjoyment were all considered to be non-Chinese, whether communist Chinese or traditional Chinese. Under this new puritanism, the moral atmosphere of which invaded political, philosophical, and social life, it was very easy to ban all foreign culture, including modern design. In the post-Mao era, m ou Chino ke  n van c he s sil br  hi wo e illi t suffer the inconvenience of old products rather than tolerate those dazzling foreign devils.

Since design has been so much ignored, there are very few product designers in China. Whenever China opens its doors to the world and enters the international market, all Chinese manufactur-f the om    o  In ya Chinese manufacturers have even gone bankrupt because of poor numbers of designers in a short time, and certainly China does not have enough good design schools. Therefore, for most manufacturers the key to survival is to imitate foreign designs that have proven successful in international markets.

After nine years in preparation, China's Industrial Design Council was finally approved by the central government and estab-T n head of China's ucler ad sace projct nd  fourstar H h o in Chinese products and expressed hope for rapid development with cooperation among engineers, artists, and designers. This in China and shows the government's awareness and positive attitude to design affairs.

In addition to meetings on matters of professional interest, the Council promotes interchanges and contacts between design schools, insti-manc Boo  s  histo theory are published by arrangements between the Council and varis publishing houses.The total number of members of the Council was 6,500 in 1988. The board of the Council consists of 77 members with an executive committee of 23 members.

The foundation of China's Design Council as the organizer o Cl    i I for design that is developing in China. Its establishment was be seen as part of a growing awareness by government leaders of n in both domestic and international markets. This awareness is evident in China's Seventh Five-Year Plan, which focuses on "gradually increasing the proportion of manufactured products in all export items" and the need to "improve the quality of commodities and strengthen their capability in competition." There is still a need for much more development and change if design is to play a full part in China's modernization, but important steps have been taken and the potential is evident.

## The Minister's brief

In 1945 the Soviet Union was returning to peacetime labor. Plants were being reconstructed to manufacture new products, closed factop elDecie   kn restore it, but to raise it to a new technical and qualitative level.

The Minister of Transport Machine Building in those years was Vyacheslav Mikhaylovich Malyshev, a highly qualified professional and an activist. He was called the country's "number one enineer." Malyshev was directy responsible for the supervision of man urgnt s.He pced bolalent people for the ob. He assigned the chief task of designing an all-metal passenger coach to the Kalinin Railway Coach Construction Plant.

Before World War II, railway coaches were made of wood, and inside were dark and small, with little windows and hard seats. Becse te tecnicl bass ws t e diffrent,the cacp

The Engineering Design Bureau at the plant understood the seriousness of the proposed work and invited Yuri Soloviev, then a young Moscow specialist, to help the department in its work.

## The railway coach design: from intuition to methodological principles

Yu. Soloviev invited two architects, Yuri Solomonovich Somov and G ical Alandovc ulak,  wtethr  the pro. Intn o t It s y d uncoob. Bu th To noe h n hs tem e en that is conduc experiments and atheranalytil data.

So they made an analysis. The upper and lower berths were completely fat. It was uncomfortable to s or lie on them, and they Ten te r ing position was useless: The berth hung along the wall, and in order to raise it, the passenger on the lower berth had to be disturbed. There was no storage for bed linen and no dining table. The lighting was dim. Ventilation was nonexistant.

As Yu. Somov recalls today, "The ideas poured out of us as if from a horn of plenty." The team rejected half of the ideas without bothering to develop them (they were too fantastic), but worthy ideas were put to strenuous test. To avoid errors, they consulted specialists.

The idea of lightening the weight of the wooden shelf, replacing it with a new lacquered panel, was checked and approved by production engineers in the aircraft industry. The designers also e h pci b uty

The designers were called craftmen in those days, as the term designer for this kind of professional was not in use in 1945.

![](images/f5ffea8d8c65dfb9ca73dfa81549c49f95868d3be387bd78b2f63357fc100335.jpg)

An essential job was performed for the ships by the craftsmen of the experimental workshops. The wood used for ships had to meet specific requirements: Wooden finishes had to withstand contrasts of temperature, humidity, moisture, and so forth. The designers concerned themselves with these aspects. The constantly trained their craftsmen at the experimental workshops to look for and evaluate the advantages of new technological processes for woodworking They therefore developed a new process of finishing by combining polishing with the application of a nitrovarnish

Thus, they ensured the required properties and the quality of wood and essentially reduced material and time expenditures. The furniture and equipment looked spectacular. But just before the aceptance of the ship b the responsible commission, somebody saw the brilliant polish on both sides of the shower doors and ecided ae them  bce e ot trust o T hnh tcoo namd Ingbe t the AAB experimental workshop, insisted on test ing this new finishing process. The shower cabins were filled with h steam three times and then exposed to cold drafts. The varnished surfaces of the doors remained smooth and brilliant on both sdes.

Design and development of the river ships significantly enriched the designers' experience. At first intuitively, then with incasing confidence they ccumulated n arsenal  professional skills and methods and worked out a strategy for cooperation with engineers and design engineers. Having the design drawings approved twice—by the engineering design bureau at Red Sormovo and by AAB—became standard practice at that time. At Red Sormovo they quickly adopted the practice of not releasing the

working drafts for production without the written authorization of the chief engineering designer and the chief architect.

There were certainly designs of AAB that ended up on the shelf. Those are now called initiative designs: They were developed pllel wih the rnt d  the ient te nvno themselves did not understand where they found the time for this work. An example of this "postponed" work was a complete reconstruction of the captain's bridge. Yu. Soloviev proposed to move it from its on-tablishe sition in the center   moor ship  e bowndo upply it with stem  T mes oproin the field of view(20 years later this solution became standard practice.)

Another example was a project for a long-distance passenger aa nt  lo nii equipment, down to a public telephone that connected a passenger with any town in the country while the train was standing in a staton."Pue fantasy,"aid se when they rejected the proj "Too soon for something like this," said others.

But these projects were not empty fantasies; they raised the morale of the designers, broadened the scope of their work, and gave them new work skills. These projects addedspice to designers' work when they undertook solutions to vital industrial tasks.

## A city trolleybus—a full-cycle design project

In 1949, a city trolleybus was designed for the Uritsky factory. This pct es tenin be t wcr mo eliab in terms of organization, methodology, and creativity. In this case, the o h r hac t T th h from scratch, which meant that time was not wasted reworking a drat. A detailed analysis of the existing prototype helped expose its shortcomings, above all those from the passengers' point of view. The analysis completed, the designers began a new elaboration of the project.
